<meta name="DC.Description" content="Carnivorous plants capture and digest animal prey for nutrition. In addition to being carnivorous, the humped bladderwort plant, Utricularia gibba , has the smallest reliably assembled flowering plant genome. We generated an updated genome assembly based on single-molecule sequencing to address questions regarding the bladderwort’s genome adaptive landscape. Among encoded genes, we segregated those that could be confidently distinguished as having derived from small-scale versus whole-genome duplication processes and showed that conspicuous expansions of gene families useful for prey trapping and processing derived mainly from localized duplication events. Such small-scale, tandem duplicates are therefore revealed as essential elements in the bladderwort’s carnivorous adaptation." />

<meta name="citation_title" content="Long-read sequencing uncovers the adaptive topography of a carnivorous plant genome" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;Utricularia gibba&lt;/i&gt;, the humped bladderwort, is a carnivorous plant that retains a tiny nuclear genome despite at least two rounds of whole genome duplication (WGD) since common ancestry with grapevine and other species. We used a third-generation genome assembly with several complete chromosomes to reconstruct the two most recent lineage-specific ancestral genomes that led to the modern &lt;i&gt;U. gibba&lt;/i&gt; genome structure. Patterns of subgenome dominance in the most recent WGD, both architectural and transcriptional, are suggestive of allopolyploidization, which may have generated genomic novelty and led to instantaneous speciation. Syntenic duplicates retained in polyploid blocks are enriched for transcription factor functions, whereas gene copies derived from ongoing tandem duplication events are enriched in metabolic functions potentially important for a carnivorous plant. Among these are tandem arrays of cysteine protease genes with trap-specific expression that evolved within a protein family known to be useful in the digestion of animal prey. Further enriched functions among tandem duplicates (also with trap-enhanced expression) include peptide transport (intercellular movement of broken-down prey proteins), ATPase activities (bladder-trap acidification and transmembrane nutrient transport), hydrolase and chitinase activities (breakdown of prey polysaccharides), and cell-wall dynamic components possibly associated with active bladder movements. Whereas independently polyploid &lt;i&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/i&gt; syntenic gene duplicates are similarly enriched for transcriptional regulatory activities, &lt;i&gt;Arabidopsis&lt;/i&gt; tandems are distinct from those of &lt;i&gt;U. gibba&lt;/i&gt;, while still metabolic and likely reflecting unique adaptations of that species. Taken together, these findings highlight the special importance of tandem duplications in the adaptive landscapes of a carnivorous plant genome.&lt;/p&gt;" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" scheme="executive-summary" content="&lt;h3&gt;Significance&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;Carnivorous plants capture and digest animal prey for nutrition. In addition to being carnivorous, the humped bladderwort plant, &lt;i&gt;Utricularia gibba&lt;/i&gt;, has the smallest reliably assembled flowering plant genome. We generated an updated genome assembly based on single-molecule sequencing to address questions regarding the bladderwort’s genome adaptive landscape. Among encoded genes, we segregated those that could be confidently distinguished as having derived from small-scale versus whole-genome duplication processes and showed that conspicuous expansions of gene families useful for prey trapping and processing derived mainly from localized duplication events. Such small-scale, tandem duplicates are therefore revealed as essential elements in the bladderwort’s carnivorous adaptation.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="Long-read sequencing uncovers the adaptive topography of a carnivorous plant genome" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://www.pnas.org/content/114/22/E4435/F1.large.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="Carnivorous plants capture and digest animal prey for nutrition. In addition to being carnivorous, the humped bladderwort plant, Utricularia gibba , has the smallest reliably assembled flowering plant genome. We generated an updated genome assembly based on single-molecule sequencing to address questions regarding the bladderwort’s genome adaptive landscape. Among encoded genes, we segregated those that could be confidently distinguished as having derived from small-scale versus whole-genome duplication processes and showed that conspicuous expansions of gene families useful for prey trapping and processing derived mainly from localized duplication events. Such small-scale, tandem duplicates are therefore revealed as essential elements in the bladderwort’s carnivorous adaptation." />

In addition to being carnivorous, the humped bladderwort plant, Utricularia gibba , has the smallest reliably assembled flowering plant genome. We generated an updated genome assembly based on single-molecule sequencing to address questions regarding the bladderwort’s genome adaptive landscape. Among encoded genes, we segregated those that could be confidently distinguished as having derived from small-scale versus whole-genome duplication processes and showed that conspicuous expansions of gene families useful for prey trapping and processing derived mainly from localized duplication events. Such small-scale, tandem duplicates are therefore revealed as essential elements in the bladderwort’s carnivorous adaptation." />

    <div class="container-fluid zone-wrapper zone-content-wrapper">
      <div class="zone zone-content row">

          <div class="region region-content col-narrow-22 col-narrow-offset-1">
    <div id="block-panels-mini-whats-new-in" class="block block-panels-mini">

    
  <div class="content">
    <div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" id="mini-panel-whats_new_in">
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-custom pane-4 whats-new-in-title" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <h2>New&nbsp;Research&nbsp;In</h2>
  </div>

  
  </div>
<div class="panel-pane pane-snippet pane-pnas-physical-sciences wni-dropdown" >
  
        <h3 class="pane-title"><span class="pane-title-text">Physical Sciences</span></h3>
    
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="pnas-physical-sciences" id="pnas-physical-sciences">
  
      
  <div class="snippet-content">
    <h4>Featured Portals</h4>

<ul class="menu">
	<li><a href="http://physics.pnas.org/">Physics</a></li>
	<li><a href="http://chemistry.pnas.org/">Chemistry</a></li>

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_pnas_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_pnas_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="executive-summary"><h2 class="">Significance</h2><p id="p-5">Carnivorous plants capture and digest animal prey for nutrition. In addition to being carnivorous, the humped bladderwort plant, <em>Utricularia gibba</em>, has the smallest reliably assembled flowering plant genome. We generated an updated genome assembly based on single-molecule sequencing to address questions regarding the bladderwort’s genome adaptive landscape. Among encoded genes, we segregated those that could be confidently distinguished as having derived from small-scale versus whole-genome duplication processes and showed that conspicuous expansions of gene families useful for prey trapping and processing derived mainly from localized duplication events. Such small-scale, tandem duplicates are therefore revealed as essential elements in the bladderwort’s carnivorous adaptation.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-6"><em>Utricularia gibba</em>, the humped bladderwort, is a carnivorous plant that retains a tiny nuclear genome despite at least two rounds of whole genome duplication (WGD) since common ancestry with grapevine and other species. We used a third-generation genome assembly with several complete chromosomes to reconstruct the two most recent lineage-specific ancestral genomes that led to the modern <em>U. gibba</em> genome structure. Patterns of subgenome dominance in the most recent WGD, both architectural and transcriptional, are suggestive of allopolyploidization, which may have generated genomic novelty and led to instantaneous speciation. Syntenic duplicates retained in polyploid blocks are enriched for transcription factor functions, whereas gene copies derived from ongoing tandem duplication events are enriched in metabolic functions potentially important for a carnivorous plant. Among these are tandem arrays of cysteine protease genes with trap-specific expression that evolved within a protein family known to be useful in the digestion of animal prey. Further enriched functions among tandem duplicates (also with trap-enhanced expression) include peptide transport (intercellular movement of broken-down prey proteins), ATPase activities (bladder-trap acidification and transmembrane nutrient transport), hydrolase and chitinase activities (breakdown of prey polysaccharides), and cell-wall dynamic components possibly associated with active bladder movements. Whereas independently polyploid <em>Arabidopsis</em> syntenic gene duplicates are similarly enriched for transcriptional regulatory activities, <em>Arabidopsis</em> tandems are distinct from those of <em>U. gibba</em>, while still metabolic and likely reflecting unique adaptations of that species. Taken together, these findings highlight the special importance of tandem duplications in the adaptive landscapes of a carnivorous plant genome.</p></div><ul class="kwd-group"><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/plant-genomics" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-plant-genomics" rel="nofollow">plant genomics</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/evolution" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-evolution" rel="nofollow">evolution</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/polyploidy" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-polyploidy" rel="nofollow">polyploidy</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/carnivorous-plant" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-carnivorous-plant" rel="nofollow">carnivorous plant</a></li><li class="kwd"><a xmlns:default="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" href="/keyword/utricularia" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-utricularia" rel="nofollow"><em xmlns:default="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml">Utricularia</em></a></li></ul><p id="p-7" class="flushleft">The architectural evolution of flowering plant genomes includes a long history of gene duplication and diversification. Tandem gene duplication is an ongoing but nonglobal process that generates coding sequence diversity in eukaryotic genomes through subfunctionalization or neofunctionalization of gene copies on an individual basis (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>). On the other hand, polyploidy events provide scores of genomically balanced duplicate genes all at once, on which divergent selection pressures can act to generate phenotypic diversity (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>, <a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>). Evidence from available plant genomes supports the theory that modular, dosage-sensitive functions such as transcriptional regulation are enriched among duplicates surviving polyploidy events, whereas single-gene survivors of local duplication events have the opportunity to be enriched for dosage responsive functions, such as secondary metabolite production (e.g., refs. <a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a><a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-5"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-6"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>). Although it has been repeatedly noted that polyploidy events correlate with some major plant radiations (<a id="xref-ref-2-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>, <a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>, <a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>), the specific roles that tandem duplicates play in species- or lineage-specific plant adaptation remain more poorly explored.</p><p id="p-8"><em>Utricularia gibba</em> is an aquatic carnivorous plant with an unusually small but highly dynamic nuclear genome that experienced at least two whole-genome duplication (WGD) events during its evolutionary history since divergence from grapevine, tomato, and other species (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>). Carnivorous plants are interesting model systems not only for understanding the molecular mechanisms underlying nutrient acquisition strategies, but also for discovering the regulatory underpinnings of their unique trapping morphologies. <em>U. gibba</em> is of particular interest given the previous publication of an ∼82-Mb short-read assembly (<a id="xref-ref-10-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>), which revealed that its genome gained and deleted gene duplicates significantly faster than those of other genomes (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>). Given that the <em>U. gibba</em> genome likely descended via considerable shrinkage from an ancestral genome up to 1.5 Gb in size (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>), duplicates that survived deletion during its evolutionary history arguably evolved under greater purifying selection pressure compared with the more expansive genomes of most angiosperms. Therefore, we hypothesized that the deletion-prone genome of <em>U. gibba</em> could be particularly illustrative regarding the adaptive legacy of differential duplicate survival following their two modes of generation, with tandems highlighting aspects of the carnivorous lifestyle and syntenic duplicates highlighting transcriptional functions.</p><p id="p-9">To explore this possibility, we generated a highly contiguous nuclear genome assembly for <em>U. gibba</em> based on Pacific Biosciences (PacBio) Single Molecule, Real-Time (SMRT) technology. We used 10 SMRT cells and P6-C4 PacBio chemistry to produce 521,937 raw and 702,640 filtered subreads with N50 values of 21,825 and 15,244 bp, respectively. After assembly with HGAP.3 (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>), we produced a genome of 581 contigs with an N50 of 3,424,836 bp and 101,949,210 total bases (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S2</a>). Remarkably, base pair correction using either the PacBio data or Illumina MiSeq reads from our previous assembly (<a id="xref-ref-10-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>) led to extremely minor improvements, only 0.071% and 0.01% of total bases, respectively (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, section 1.5</a>). Four contigs represented complete chromosomes marked on either end by telomeres, including the longest contig of the assembly at 8,502,017 bp (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>). Twenty additional contigs had telomere repeats on one end, the 14 largest being ≥1 Mb in size (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>). <em>Arabidopsis</em>-type telomeric repeats (TTTAGGG) were identified in these 24 contigs. Two variants, the <em>Chlamydomonas</em> type (<a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">14</a>) (TTTTTAGGG) and TTCAGGG (similar to the variants TTCAGG and TTTCAGG known from the close carnivorous plant relative <em>Genlisea</em>) (<a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>), were also found sporadically intermingled with the <em>Arabidopsis</em>-type telomeric repeats. Ten contigs were observed to have interstitial telomeric repeats, which were identified by searching for (CCCTAAA)<sub>3</sub> and (TTTAGGG)<sub>3</sub> within chromosomal arms (<a id="xref-fig-1-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>A</em></a>). After filtration for bacterial and other contamination <em>(</em><a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, section 1.6</a>), the assembled genome amounted to 100,688,548 bp (on 518 contigs), including a complete 172,489-bp plastid genome on a single contig and a 283,823-bp partial mitochondrial genome (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, section 1.6.2</a>). Therefore, our newly assembled nuclear genome gained 18,356,750 bp from the former assembly size of 81,875,486 bp.</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-featured  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/22/E4435/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="A chromosome-scale view of the architecture of the U. gibba genome. (A) Gene density, TE density tracks, telomeres, and the locations of CRM centromeric retrotransposon sequences are shown for all U. gibba contigs &gt;1 Mb in size. Four complete chromosomal contigs are shown in blue, and partial chromosomes that have at least one end with telomere sequence are shown in orange. Putative centromeric regions are visible as peaks of increased TE density and decreased gene density. Most CRMs are localized at putative centromeric regions. (B) MUMmer (82) pairwise dot-plot alignment of contigs 0 and 22, which represent complete chromosomes. Blue and purple dots indicate hits on each DNA strand, respectively. Putative centromeric regions of strong sequence similarity are apparent as a densely hit square." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-23994381" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;A chromosome-scale view of the architecture of the &lt;em&gt;U. gibba&lt;/em&gt; genome. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) Gene density, TE density tracks, telomeres, and the locations of CRM centromeric retrotransposon sequences are shown for all &lt;em&gt;U. gibba&lt;/em&gt; contigs &gt;1 Mb in size. Four complete chromosomal contigs are shown in blue, and partial chromosomes that have at least one end with telomere sequence are shown in orange. Putative centromeric regions are visible as peaks of increased TE density and decreased gene density. Most CRMs are localized at putative centromeric regions. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) MUMmer (82) pairwise dot-plot alignment of contigs 0 and 22, which represent complete chromosomes. Blue and purple dots indicate hits on each DNA strand, respectively. Putative centromeric regions of strong sequence similarity are apparent as a densely hit square.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/22/E4435/F1.medium.gif" width="262" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 1." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/22/E4435/F1.medium.gif" width="262" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/22/E4435/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/22/E4435/F1.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/17869" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 1.</span> <p id="p-10" class="first-child">A chromosome-scale view of the architecture of the <em>U. gibba</em> genome. (<em>A</em>) Gene density, TE density tracks, telomeres, and the locations of CRM centromeric retrotransposon sequences are shown for all <em>U. gibba</em> contigs &gt;1 Mb in size. Four complete chromosomal contigs are shown in blue, and partial chromosomes that have at least one end with telomere sequence are shown in orange. Putative centromeric regions are visible as peaks of increased TE density and decreased gene density. Most CRMs are localized at putative centromeric regions. (<em>B</em>) MUMmer (<a id="xref-ref-82-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-82">82</a>) pairwise dot-plot alignment of contigs 0 and 22, which represent complete chromosomes. Blue and purple dots indicate hits on each DNA strand, respectively. Putative centromeric regions of strong sequence similarity are apparent as a densely hit square.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-11">Calculation of the genome space occupied by transposable elements (TEs) uncovered almost 9 Mb (∼8.9%) complete TEs, with up to 59 Mb (∼59%) of the nuclear genome possibly TE-derived (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd01.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>); the latter amounted to ∼16.6 Mb more TE-related genome space than was found in the previously published short-read assembly (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, section 2.1</a>). We found that ∼2.9 Mb of the genome (on 115 contigs) was composed of ribosomal DNA repeats (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, section 2.2</a>). Indeed, a syntenic path alignment with the short-read assembly demonstrated that most of the DNA gained by PacBio sequencing contained repeated elements, particularly surrounding putative centromeres (<a id="xref-fig-1-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>B</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S4–S8</a>).</p><p id="p-12">To identify signature centromeric repeats in <em>U. gibba</em>, we selected tandem repeat clusters with average period size of 50–500 bp for identification as putative centromere repeats (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S5<em>B</em></a>), as described previously (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>). The top 10 most abundant tandem repeat clusters were considered prime candidates for centromeric repeats, but these were not even preferentially located in our chromosome-sized contigs. We then manually checked the locations of the next 10 most abundant tandem repeat clusters in the genome, and found that none of these clusters showed unique localization in putative centromeric regions. Therefore, we conclude that <em>U. gibba</em> centromeres are devoid of high-copy tandem repeat arrays such as those known from <em>Arabidopsis</em> and maize (<a id="xref-ref-16-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>). Similar findings also have been reported for the centromeres of several plant and animal species (<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a><a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-18"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>), including two closely related carnivorous plants, <em>Genlisea hispidula</em> and <em>Genlisea subglabra (<a id="xref-ref-15-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>)</em>.</p><p id="p-13">Although plant retrotransposon families generally are randomly dispersed, there are families distinctly concentrated in centromeric regions, such as the CRM centromeric chromoviruses. CRMs, a lineage of <em>Ty3/gypsy</em> retrotransposons, have been well characterized as centromeric retrotransposons in many species (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a><a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-21"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-22"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-23"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-24"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a>), including <em>G. hispidula</em> and <em>G. subglabra (<a id="xref-ref-15-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>)</em>. Using phylogenetic analysis, we found that 55 <em>U. gibba</em> sequences are grouped within the subgroup A CRMs, which include the centromere-specific CRMs (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, section 3.3.3</a>). All but one of the <em>U. gibba</em> sequences form a single, monophyletic CRM subfamily. To investigate the chromosomal localization of the 55 <em>U. gibba</em> CRMs, we plotted them on the complete and near-complete chromosomes together with the TE and gene model tracks. As depicted in <a id="xref-fig-1-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>A</em></a>, most <em>U. gibba</em> CRMs are located in the putative centromeric regions; however, not all putative centromeres have CRM elements. It has been proposed that CRMs may play an important role in stabilizing centromere structure and maintaining centromere function (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>, <a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>), whereas an opposing hypothesis holds that they are merely parasitic and tend to accumulate in recombination-poor centromeric regions to escape negative selection against insertions in distal regions (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>). Our finding that some putative centromeric regions in <em>U. gibba</em> lack CRMs or other high-copy centromeric tandem repeats suggests that neither CRMs nor tandem repeats are crucial for maintaining functional centromeres in the species.</p><p id="p-14">Our highly contiguous genome assembly also permitted a much finer account of protein-coding gene number than previously available, which amounted to 30,689, 7.7% more than reported for our short-read assembly (<a id="xref-ref-10-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>). Unlike the far shorter scaffolds from that assembly (<a id="xref-ref-10-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>), our largely chromosome-sized contigs permitted us to conservatively distinguish the WGD-derived and tandem duplicate portions of <em>U. gibba</em>’s genome adaptive landscape. In both cases, we were concerned with duplicates that could still be discerned within their formative genome structural contexts, not with duplicates that might have migrated to other chromosomal positions after their generation via small-scale or WGD events, because such genes could be only indirectly assigned to one duplicative process versus the other.</p><p id="p-15">Through syntenic analysis using CoGe (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>, <a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>), we were able to identify 54 syntenic block pairs descending from the most recent <em>U. gibba</em> WGD event (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S11</a>). We were then able to reconstruct the immediate, nine-chromosome prepolyploid ancestor of the modern genome, following which numerous large-scale inversion events were required to account for modern gene order (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, section 4.1</a>). Further analysis permitted deconstruction of this ancestral genome into an earlier, six-chromosome pre-WGD ancestor that existed immediately before <em>U. gibba</em>’s second most recent polyploidy event (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S12</a>); however, we could not reconstruct the third WGD event that was previously described based on visual inspection of syntenic dot plots and syntenic depth calculations (<a id="xref-ref-10-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>). Nonetheless, microsynteny analyses did reveal many examples of eight (or more)-to-one syntenic block relationships with the <em>Vitis vinifera</em> genome (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, section 4.5</a>), some of which may include blocks dating to the gamma hexaploidy event at the base of all core eudicots (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>).</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/22/E4435/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Syntenic relationships among V. vinifera, S. lycopersicum, and U. gibba regions containing tandemly duplicated cysteine protease genes. Some parts of these tandem arrays clearly preexisted in U. gibba&#x2019;s prepolyploid ancestral genomes, with further tandem duplications having occurred since those events, together increasing functional potential for U. gibba&#x2019;s carnivory. A typical ancestral region in Vitis can be traced to up to three regions in Solanum (through the latter's genome triplication) and up to eight regions in U. gibba (where as many as three WGDs are possible). Red connecting lines highlight matching cysteine proteases in the selected regions; genes otherwise syntenic are shown in gray." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-23994381" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Syntenic relationships among &lt;em&gt;V. vinifera&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;S. lycopersicum&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;U. gibba&lt;/em&gt; regions containing tandemly duplicated cysteine protease genes. Some parts of these tandem arrays clearly preexisted in &lt;em&gt;U. gibba&lt;/em&gt;&#x2019;s prepolyploid ancestral genomes, with further tandem duplications having occurred since those events, together increasing functional potential for &lt;em&gt;U. gibba&lt;/em&gt;&#x2019;s carnivory. A typical ancestral region in &lt;em&gt;Vitis&lt;/em&gt; can be traced to up to three regions in &lt;em&gt;Solanum&lt;/em&gt; (through the latter's genome triplication) and up to eight regions in &lt;em&gt;U. gibba&lt;/em&gt; (where as many as three WGDs are possible). Red connecting lines highlight matching cysteine proteases in the selected regions; genes otherwise syntenic are shown in gray.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 2." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/22/E4435/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="385"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 2." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/22/E4435/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="385"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/22/E4435/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 2." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/22/E4435/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/17871" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 2.</span> <p id="p-16" class="first-child">Syntenic relationships among <em>V. vinifera</em>, <em>S. lycopersicum</em>, and <em>U. gibba</em> regions containing tandemly duplicated cysteine protease genes. Some parts of these tandem arrays clearly preexisted in <em>U. gibba</em>’s prepolyploid ancestral genomes, with further tandem duplications having occurred since those events, together increasing functional potential for <em>U. gibba</em>’s carnivory. A typical ancestral region in <em>Vitis</em> can be traced to up to three regions in <em>Solanum</em> (through the latter's genome triplication) and up to eight regions in <em>U. gibba</em> (where as many as three WGDs are possible). Red connecting lines highlight matching cysteine proteases in the selected regions; genes otherwise syntenic are shown in gray.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-17">We analyzed the duplicate block pairs from the most recent WGD event to assess the degree of fractionation (gene loss) experienced by each subgenome following polyploidization (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S13</a>). This analysis yielded a clear pattern of deletion bias characteristic of subgenome dominance inherited through a polyploidy event (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>, <a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>). Fractionation bias was matched by both subgenome expression dominance (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>) and fewer single nucleotide polymorphisms on dominant blocks (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>, <a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, section 4.4, Figs. S13 and S14</a>, and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd03.xlsx" class="in-nw">Datasets S3</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd04.xlsx" class="in-nw">S4</a>), indicating the influence of stronger purifying selection. Taken together, these data suggest that the most recent WGD in <em>U. gibba</em>’s past was an allopolyploidization event resulting from a broad cross (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>), because autopolyploidies are not expected to show such strong biases; for example, unbiased fractionation has been discovered in the genomes of poplar, banana, and soybean (<a id="xref-ref-37-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>, <a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">38</a>). Hybridization of two species accompanied by genome doubling can instantly generate a third species with novel and transcendent phenotypic traits (<a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">39</a>). Moreover, the modern <em>U. gibba</em> genome displays highly heterogeneous patterns of heterozygosity (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd04.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S4</a>) that do not correlate with the structural limits of syntenic blocks, suggesting that outcrossing events subsequent to the most recent WGD were broad, but were not followed by ploidy changes. Given the highly clonal nature of aquatic <em>Utricularia</em> species (e.g., refs. <a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">40</a>, <a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">41</a>), this state could represent “frozen” heterozygosity in a particularly adaptive genotype, such as seen in unisexual hybrid vertebrates (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">42</a>).</p><p id="p-18">To examine polyploid adaptive genetic features in <em>U. gibba</em>, we evaluated gene ontology (GO) functional enrichments among syntenically retained gene duplicates descending from <em>U. gibba</em>’s lineage-specific WGDs. Duplicates retained following WGD were mostly enriched for transcriptional regulatory functions (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd05.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>). As expected based on earlier studies, very similar results were obtained for <em>Arabidopsis</em> WGD duplicates analyzed in the same manner (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd06.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S6</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-4-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>, <a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">43</a>, <a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">44</a>); however, comparing the 522 <em>U. gibba</em> WGD duplicates annotated with the GO “regulation of transcription, DNA-templated” with all <em>U. gibba</em> genes with this GO revealed no significant enrichment of any biological process category (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd14.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S14</a>). Similar analysis of <em>Arabidopsis</em> WGD duplicates yielded only one significant biological process category, “response to jasmonic acid” (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd15.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S15</a>), suggesting that in both species, transcriptional regulatory enrichment is functionally generic.</p><p id="p-19">In contrast to functional enrichments of WGD duplicates, <em>U. gibba</em> genes filtered out by the blast_to_raw script in the QUOTA-ALIGN package [<a href="https://github.com/tanghaibao/quota-alignment" class="in-nw">https://github.com/tanghaibao/quota-alignment</a> (<a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">45</a>), included in CoGe SynMap (<a id="xref-ref-29-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>, <a id="xref-ref-30-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>)] as tandem duplicates in the modern genome (and thus ignored in syntenic dot plot comparison) were enriched for many secondary metabolic functions, including specific functions that could be anticipated for a carnivorous plant (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd07.xlsx" class="in-nw">Datasets S7</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd08.xlsx" class="in-nw">S8</a>). <em>Arabidopsis</em> tandems discovered in the same manner were similarly enriched for secondary metabolic activities, as anticipated based on earlier results (<a id="xref-ref-5-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>). However, in many cases the <em>Arabidopsis</em> activities were entirely different (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd09.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S9</a>). Among the most significantly enriched categories in <em>U. gibba</em> was the category “oligopeptide transporter activity,” assigned to 23 members of the OPT gene family (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">46</a>). Importantly, oligopeptide transport was also among the most significantly enriched functional categories of genes specifically and strongly expressed in the bladder traps (<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>), with 13 genes showing 4- to 400-fold trap-enhanced transcription (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd08.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S8</a>). Peptide transporters, which are involved in the plant nitrogen budget, have been identified as expressed in the trap fluid of the carnivorous pitcher plant <em>Nepenthes</em> (<a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>, <a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">49</a>). The <em>Nepenthes</em> gene identified in that study is, however, a member of the PTR family, a group itself highlighted among <em>U. gibba</em> tandems by the significantly enriched term “dipeptide transporter activity,” wherein there are 22 family members, including three homologs of the <em>Arabidopsis</em> nitrate transporter gene <em>NPF5.5</em> (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>); <em>unitig_52.g17408.t1</em> and <em>unitig_26.g9035.t1</em> had &gt;65-fold trap-enhanced expression (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd08.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S8</a>). Carnivorous plants, bladderworts included, typically grow in nitrogen-poor habitats, where they compensate for deficiencies via prey capture and uptake of released nitrogen.</p><p id="p-20">Another highly enriched functional category among tandem duplicates was “ATPase activity, coupled to transmembrane movement of substances,” comprising 58 genes, mostly ABC transporters. Proteins encoded by such genes are known from <em>Nepenthes</em> traps, where they are hypothesized to be responsible for maintaining trap acidity and various molecular transport functions (<a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>). Several of these genes show greater than ninefold trap-specific expression, including <em>unitig_85.g27344.t1</em>, <em>unitig_85.g27345.t1</em>, <em>unitig_750.g28500.t1</em>, and <em>unitig_750.g28501.t1</em> (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd08.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S8</a>). Another enriched category was “transmembrane transport,” which highlighted all of the foregoing genes and also included eight phosphate transporter genes homologous to <em>PHT1</em> (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>). PHT1 family genes are induced during nutritional phosphate deficiency, a condition characteristic of the carnivorous plant lifestyle (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>). Of these, <em>unitig_747.g21685.t1</em> and <em>unitig_747.g21690.t1</em> showed 2- to 24-fold trap-enhanced expression (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd08.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S8</a>).</p><p id="p-21">Another significantly enriched tandem duplicate functional category was “hydrolase activity, hydrolyzing O-glycosyl compounds.” This GO category included a gene encoding a class III chitinase (<em>unitig_60.g25630.t1</em>, showing &gt;20-fold trap-enhanced expression) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd08.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S8</a>), representing one of the chitinase families [glycoside hydrolase (GH) family 18] active within the digestive fluid of both open and closed traps of various carnivorous plant species. In <em>Nepenthes</em>, the GH family 18 enzyme is encoded by a single-copy gene that is up-regulated in response to prey in both the pitted glands and surrounding tissues (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>). Galactosidases and xylosidases (<a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>) are also among the genes with the hydrolase annotation, and enzymes encoding both have been identified in the <em>Nepenthes</em> trap fluid proteome (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">56</a>, <a id="xref-ref-57-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">57</a>). <em>Nepenthes</em> and <em>Drosera</em> (carnivorous sundew plant) digestive mucilage contains galactose and xylose (<a id="xref-ref-58-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-58">58</a>), which may require breakdown for peptide and other nutrient absorption in <em>U. gibba</em> traps as well (<a id="xref-ref-59-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-59">59</a>). Three xylosidase genes—<em>unitig_62.g23624.t1</em>, <em>unitig_62.g23625.t1</em>, and <em>unitig_748.g7352.t1</em>—show 4- to 35-fold trap-enhanced expression (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd08.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S8</a>).</p><p id="p-22">The traps of <em>Utricularia</em> operate through an intricate triggering mechanism (<a id="xref-ref-60-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-60">60</a>). High-speed snap-buckling movements (<a id="xref-ref-61-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-61">61</a>, <a id="xref-ref-62-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">62</a>) occur following triggered release of negative internal trap pressure achieved by active pumping out of water (<a id="xref-ref-63-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-63">63</a>). Prey is engulfed with the influx of liquid, after which the trap may reset itself with a new negative pressure potential. This repeating process likely demands highly dynamic cell-wall changes. Indeed, the tandems-enriched GO category “cell wall” annotated 17 genes encoding expansins (<a id="xref-ref-64-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-64">64</a>) (none of which, however, showed uniformly trap-enriched expression) and 8 genes encoding xyloglucan endotransglycosylases (<a id="xref-ref-65-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-65">65</a>) (of which <em>unitig_749.g14196.t1</em> and <em>unitig_26.g9135.t1</em> showed greater than sixfold trap-enhanced expression) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd08.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S8</a>). Seventeen encoded peroxidases homologous to PRX52, which cross-link cell-wall strengthening extensins (<em>unitig_26.g8978.t1</em> and <em>unitig_22.g6605.t1</em> were &gt;14-fold trap-enhanced), and 21 encoded polygalacturonases, which degrade cell-wall pectin (<a id="xref-ref-66-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-66">66</a>) (<em>unitig_8.g3155.t1</em> and <em>unitig_8.g3156.t1</em> were &gt;fourfold trap-enhanced) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd08.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S8</a>). Indeed, members of these protein families have been identified as candidates for involvement in plant mechanical stimulation or movements (<a id="xref-ref-62-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-62">62</a>, <a id="xref-ref-67-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-67">67</a>, <a id="xref-ref-68-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-68">68</a>). Another cell-wall modification-related gene family under this GO term encoded a group of 19 pectin methylesterases and their inhibitors (<a id="xref-ref-69-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-69">69</a>) (<em>unitig_899.g15179.t1</em> and <em>unitig_22.g5384.t1</em> were 2- to 32-fold trap-enhanced) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd08.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S8</a>). Interestingly, a second class of chitinases, the class IV enzymes, was also highlighted as an expanded gene family under the GO category “cell wall,” but none of these five genes showed trap-enhanced expression. Class IV chitinases are defense response proteins that represent a second family of chitinase (GH family 19) involved in plant carnivory (<a id="xref-ref-70-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">70</a>, <a id="xref-ref-71-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-71">71</a>). Finally, four genes encoding β-galactosidases (known from <em>Nepenthes</em> pitcher fluid) (<a id="xref-ref-57-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">57</a>) appeared under the same GO category but did not have trap-enhanced expression in <em>U. gibba.</em> Another expanded GO category, “lipid catabolic process,” comprised members of various lipase gene families, among them genes encoding patatin-like and GDSL lipases (<em>unitig_736.g22657.t1</em>, <em>unitig_37.g12702.t1</em>, <em>unitig_736.g22658.t1</em>, and <em>unitig_37.g12699.t1</em> showed 35- to 180-fold trap-enhanced expression) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd08.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S8</a>). A GDSL lipase likely related to carnivory was identified in the trap fluid of <em>Nepenthes</em> pitchers (<a id="xref-ref-57-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-57">57</a>).</p><p id="p-23">Strikingly, the most significantly enriched GO category among all tandemly duplicated genes, “senescence-associated vacuole,” pointed to a specific expansion in one gene family encoding cysteine proteases that had nearly trap-specific expression patterns (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd02.xlsx" class="in-nw">Datasets S2</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd08.xlsx" class="in-nw">S8</a>). Several other significantly enriched GOs are associated with this gene family. Cysteine proteases have been identified as major functional components of Venus flytrap (<em>Dionaea muscipula</em>) digestive fluid (<a id="xref-ref-72-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-72">72</a>), reported in three <em>D. muscipula</em> transcriptomes (<a id="xref-ref-70-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-70">70</a>, <a id="xref-ref-73-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-73">73</a>, <a id="xref-ref-74-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-74">74</a>), and structurally annotated for both Cape sundew (<em>Drosera capensis</em>) draft genome sequences (<a id="xref-ref-75-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-75">75</a>, <a id="xref-ref-76-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-76">76</a>) and <em>D. muscipula</em> (<a id="xref-ref-77-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-77">77</a>). We found tandem clusters of homologous protease-encoding genes in the <em>U. gibba</em> genome that had demonstrably undergone tandem duplication both before and after the most recent WGD event in <em>U. gibba</em>’s evolutionary history (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a>). These tandem cysteine protease arrays are assignable to both dominant and recessive subgenomic blocks and are more preserved on the dominant block, where enhanced purifying selection on gene space is expected (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S13</a>). Genome-wide BLAST search revealed that in general, <em>U. gibba</em> cysteine proteases have become nearly totally restricted to this single, specific subfamily, clearly indicating that diverse, related cysteine proteases known from various other species have become expendable during <em>U. gibba</em>’s genome evolution.</p><p id="p-24">We further examined the cysteine proteases for molecular evolutionary features (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, section 6.1</a>), given that gene family members would have diversified in sequence and function to be retained by selection in the dynamically shrinking <em>U. gibba</em> genome. The alternative would be that the observed duplicates were extremely recent and functionally redundant; however, analyses of protein evolution showed this to not be the case, although tandem duplications did continue following the most recent WGD event that yielded arrays on contigs 85 and 699 (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>A</em></a>). Instead, we detected evidence for positive selection acting on specific amino acid residues in a lineage leading to several of the <em>U. gibba</em> cysteine protease duplicates (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>A</em></a>). When homology modeling these changes onto the <em>D. muscipula</em> cysteine protease structure (<a id="xref-ref-77-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-77">77</a>) (Protein Data Bank ID code 5a24), we found some of these amino acids located within the substrate-binding cleft, near residues with known functions in protease activity (<a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3 <em>B</em> and <em>C</em></a>). These substitutions could affect polarity and charge within the cleft, as well as hydrogen bonding between residues essential for catalytic activity and the ligand.</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/22/E4435/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Molecular and structural evolutionary analysis of U. gibba cysteine proteases suggests adaptive protein evolution accompanying WGD and tandem duplication events. (A) Best-scoring tree from maximum-likelihood based searches, with bootstrap support (BS) values &#x2265;50 indicated at branches. Symbols on branches indicate significant evidence for positive selection (orange stars), divergent selection (green circles), or asymmetrical sequence evolution (purple hexagons) as determined using PAML (83) (SI Appendix, Dataset S10). The heatmap above the phylogeny shows trap-dominant expression of particular homologs in U. gibba, based on trap, shoot, and inflorescence transcriptome data (47) (SI Appendix, Dataset S2). Note that two tandem duplicates (g1 and g2) were repredicted at locus utg699.g19345. (B) The protein homology surface model for the catalytic domain of utg699.g19348 (encoded by the gene annotated by an arrow in A; based on the Venus flytrap [D. muscipula] enzyme structure (77)) shows that some residues under positive selection lie within or near the substrate-binding cleft. The cleft is depicted in yellow, and amino acid sites identified as under positive selection are indicated in red or cyan. Three (E24, V69, and S160) amino acid sites under positive selection (BEB confidence &gt;0.82, Bonferroni-corrected P &lt; 0.0015) are within five amino acids of known D. muscipula functional residues, where they line the substrate-binding cleft (red). (C) Plot of utg699.g19348 amino acid sites under positive selection, with colors corresponding to specific sites in the surface model (SI Appendix, Fig. S4B)." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-23994381" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Molecular and structural evolutionary analysis of &lt;em&gt;U. gibba&lt;/em&gt; cysteine proteases suggests adaptive protein evolution accompanying WGD and tandem duplication events. <li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/114/22/E4435/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/17849" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 3.</span> <p id="p-25" class="first-child">Molecular and structural evolutionary analysis of <em>U. gibba</em> cysteine proteases suggests adaptive protein evolution accompanying WGD and tandem duplication events. (<em>A</em>) Best-scoring tree from maximum-likelihood based searches, with bootstrap support (BS) values ≥50 indicated at branches. Symbols on branches indicate significant evidence for positive selection (orange stars), divergent selection (green circles), or asymmetrical sequence evolution (purple hexagons) as determined using PAML (<a id="xref-ref-83-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-83">83</a>) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd10.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S10</a>). The heatmap above the phylogeny shows trap-dominant expression of particular homologs in <em>U. gibba</em>, based on trap, shoot, and inflorescence transcriptome data (<a id="xref-ref-47-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd02.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>). Note that two tandem duplicates (g1 and g2) were repredicted at locus <em>utg699.g19345</em>. (<em>B</em>) The protein homology surface model for the catalytic domain of utg699.g19348 (encoded by the gene annotated by an arrow in <em>A</em>; based on the Venus flytrap [<em>D. muscipula</em>] enzyme structure (<a id="xref-ref-77-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-77">77</a>)) shows that some residues under positive selection lie within or near the substrate-binding cleft. The cleft is depicted in yellow, and amino acid sites identified as under positive selection are indicated in red or cyan. Three (E24, V69, and S160) amino acid sites under positive selection (BEB confidence &gt;0.82, Bonferroni-corrected <em>P</em> &lt; 0.0015) are within five amino acids of known <em>D. muscipula</em> functional residues, where they line the substrate-binding cleft (red). (<em>C</em>) Plot of utg699.g19348 amino acid sites under positive selection, with colors corresponding to specific sites in the surface model (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S4<em>B</em></a>).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-26"><em>SHORT VEGETATIVE PHASE</em> (<em>SVP</em>) MADS box gene homologs and homologs of the cuticle biosynthesis gene <em>3-KETOACYL-COA SYNTHASE 6</em> (<em>KCS6</em>; highlighted by the significantly enriched GO category among tandems, “wax biosynthetic process”) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd08.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S8</a>) are two additional cases of tandem duplicate arrays for which some members exhibit trap-enhanced gene expression. Both of these examples have been described previously, based on simple orthogroup clustering methods, as generic gene family expansions derived from unknown duplication mechanisms (<a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>). However, only our highly contiguous PacBio genome provides the structural context necessary to discern that these duplicates are tandems. The <em>SVP</em>-like gene cluster may be involved with flowering phenology, and the <em>KCS6</em>-like genes may be involved in cuticle buttressing of the thin, two-celled trap wall (<a id="xref-ref-78-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-78">78</a><a id="xref-ref-79-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-79"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-80-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-80">80</a>). The <em>SVP</em>-like genes appear to have diversified anciently, whereas the <em>KCS6</em>-like array occurs in a region of the genome without internal synteny, so it is likely more recent than the last <em>U. gibba</em> WGD. Similar to the cysteine protease clusters, we discovered likely evidence of protein functional divergence in both of these array types (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd10.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S10</a>). Also of note, both the cysteine protease and <em>KCS6</em>-like gene clusters occur within islands of mobile elements (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, section 2.5</a>) annotated as large retrotransposon derivatives (LARDs) (<a id="xref-ref-81-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-81">81</a>). Serving as a good illustration of the repeat discovery power of PacBio sequencing, ∼47% of the total TE assembly space comprised LARDs, whereas these elements amounted to only ∼14.6% of TEs in the previous short-read assembly (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sd01.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>). We hypothesize that LARDs and other DNA repeats may have facilitated the tandem duplications that gave rise to metabolic gene arrays, as illustrated in the foregoing examples. Finally, we hypothesize that such tandem gene clusters could be coregulated to act in concert, perhaps at particular plant developmental stages or under particular environmental stimuli.</p><p id="p-27">Taken together, our findings regarding the size-limited <em>U. gibba</em> genome highlight the important role that tandemly duplicated genes, under sufficiently substantial purifying selection to survive continual deletion pressure, may play in the individualized adaptive genomic architecture of a plant uniquely adapted for carnivorous morphology and physiology. Although WGD duplicates are not enriched for such niche-specific functions, polyploidy events clearly potentiated the evolutionary influence of preexisting tandem arrays.</p><div class="section materials-methods" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">Materials and Methods</h2><p id="p-28" class="flushleft"><em>U. gibba</em> material was sourced from Umécuaro municipality, Michoacán, México, and grown in sterile tissue culture before nuclear DNA extraction. DNA was sequenced using PacBio SMRT technology and assembled using HGAP.3. Genome features were then annotated and analyzed using various bioinformatic tools. GO enrichments were analyzed within different gene pools. For selected gene families, molecular evolutionary pressures were evaluated using codon models and likelihood ratio tests. Detailed information is provided in <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1702072114/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1702072114.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a>.</p></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-29">We thank Thomas J. Givnish for an insightful additional review. Funding for this work was provided by National Science Foundation Grants 0922742 and 1442190 (to V.A.A.).</p></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><h2>Footnotes</h2><ul class="pnas-footnotes"><li class="corresp" id="corresp-1"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-corresp-1-1">↵</a><sup>1</sup>To whom correspondence may be addressed. <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="12"><span class="nlm-given-names">Stephan C.</span> <span class="nlm-surname">Schuster</span></span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="13"><span class="nlm-given-names">Luis</span> <span class="nlm-surname">Herrera-Estrella</span></span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="14"><span class="nlm-given-names">Victor A.</span> <span class="nlm-surname">Albert</span></span></span></div>
  
    	<div class="highwire-cite-metadata"><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-oa-ind highwire-cite-metadata"><i class="highwire-oa-indicator"></i> </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-journal highwire-cite-metadata">Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-date highwire-cite-metadata">May 2017, </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-volume highwire-cite-metadata">114 </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-issue highwire-cite-metadata">(22) </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-pages highwire-cite-metadata">E4435-E4441; </span><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-doi highwire-cite-metadata"><span class="label">DOI:</span> 10.1073/pnas.1702072114 </span></div>
